DTU
Abstract
DTU Compute
Modelling of epidemics using stochastic dierential equations with
varying test intensity
by Thor Grønborg Junker
The COVID-19 epidemic has big societal and economic impact on the world and
Denmark. It is of great interest to people, mink and politicians alike, to gain additional
insight into the current state of the epidemic.
We use a mixed eects model to estimate a relative prevalence and reproduction
number of SARS-CoV-2 in Denmark. The infection rate is considered a stochastic
progress to capture governmental regulations and change in peoples behaviour. The
relative prevalence is used to illustrate the development of COVID-19 in Denmark.
Currently, (d.16 April 2021) the reproduction number is estimated to be 1.017, thus
the epidemic is expected to slightly increase. We also found the transmission rate of
B.1.1.7 to be 34% higher than the local wild types.
==PAGE:6==
vi
DTU
Abstract - Danish
DTU Compute
Modelling of epidemics using stochastic dierential equations with
varying test intensity
by Thor Grønborg Junker
COVID-19 pandemien har stor samfundsmæssig og økonomisk betydning på både
Danmark og resten af verden. Der er derfor af stor interesse fra både borgere, mink
og politikere, I at få et bedre indblik i udviklingen af epidemien. Vi bruger en mixed
eects model til, at estimere et udtryk for prævalens og reproduktionstal af SARS-
CoV-2 i Danmark. Vi antager, at infektionsraten følger en stokastisk process med
henblik på, at følge regerings restriktioner og ændringer i folks adfærd. Den relative
prævalens bruges til, at visualisere udvikling af COVID-19 i Danmark over de sidste
14 måneder. Det seneste estimate (d. 16 April) af reproduktionstallet er 1.017, det er
derfor forventligt at epidemien stiger langsomt. Smitten raten af B.1.1.7 er estimeret
til at være 34% højere end de resterende varianter i Danmark.

1
Chapter 1
Introduction
Severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus 2 (SARS-CoV-2), also called corona, is
a virus that causes respiratory illness and is responsible for the global COVID-19 pan-
demic. The virus originates from a local seafood market in Wuhan, Hubei province, in
central China and was rst discovered on December 12 2019 (Zhou et al. 2020). From
there the virus quickly spread and as of April 11 2021, there has been 135,467,026
conrmed cases of SARS-CoV-2 globally and 2,929,837 deaths1.
The rst conrmed case of SARS-CoV-2 in Denmark was recorded at February 26
20202 and since then there have been 236.346 conrmed cases and 2436 have died
af as result3. In addition, the total cost of the Corona epidemic and the multiple
restriction measures that were established to reduce transmissibility is estimated to
have cost Denmark 536 Billion dkk4.
With control measures having substantial societal and/or economic impact, it is es-
sential to justify the necessity with accurate evidence.
However, if the estimates
are unreliable due to inaccuracies in underlying model denitions or systematic and
time-variant biases in the collection of data, it becomes hard to decide when control
measures should be implemented to stop the spread.
In addition, to make accurately real-time estimates, it is benecial to consider past
observations and track changes in growth of infections. This can be used to assess
the eectiveness of past control measures and allow for more optimal decisions in the
future.
The most intuitive way of dening the burden of infection is with the true prevalence
i.e the portion of population that are infected. This also makes predictions easier since
the growth of infections is dependent on the current portion of infected. However, the
start and end of each infection is unknown and data will only consist of single time
point observations. The prevalence will therefore be based on inference over incidence
observations.
The most relevant data source is the daily number of positives as it provides the
most immediate information. If the testing strategy was randomized so people were
selected randomly across the population, then the positive precent would serve as a
good estimate of prevalence. Unfortunately, they are not. Every test is motivated
with dierent reasons: Symptoms, contact tracing, need for travel or gatherings, cu-
riosity etc. And cannot be expected to remain constant either spatially or temporally.
Furthermore, the test intensity has increased substantially during the Covid 19 epi-
demic. In the beginning, the number of tests was limited by capacity and focused on
1https://www.who.int/emergencies/diseases/novel-coronavirus-2019/situation-reports
2https://www.ssi.dk/aktuelt/nyheder/2020/02_27_foerste-tilfaelde-af-ny-coronavirus-i-dk
3https://www.sst.dk/en/english/corona-eng/status-of-the-epidemic/
covid-19-updates-statistics-and-charts
4https://blog.rangvid.com/2021/01/10/the-cost-of-the-second-wave/
==PAGE:10==
2
Chapter 1. Introduction
symptomatic individuals where it is now limited by the number that feel motivated.
So it is expected that number of positives is connected to the number of tests in a
non-linear fashion.
Another source of data is hospitalizations that are caused by SARS-CoV-2. These
are a direct measure of the severity which is the factor that controls the need for
regulations. They also does not dependent on testing strategy and might therefore be
a better measure of the underlying prevalence. However, they are exposed to lag and
they are only a small number which makes uctuations more prevalent.
The time-dependent reproduction number Rt (The average number of new infections
caused by one infectious host) is a useful parameter to assess whether an epidemic
is under control or not. As a result, a lot of research has been put into estimating
the time-varying reproduction number (Flaxman et al. 2020; Bettencourt and Ribeiro
2008; Cori et al. 2013; Dietz 1993; Thompson et al. 2019). However only little focus
has been put on temporal eects of model parameters. This issue is usually solved by
assuming piecewise constant parameters in rolling windows. So the parameters are
estimated on a subset of data which is then moved and the parameters re-estimated.
Thompson et al. 2019.
The goal of this thesis is to do inference on the current state of the epidemic by
incorporating multiple sources of information. We seek to construct a grey-box model
which is a model that is partly driven by data/observations but also rely on the phys-
ical properties of the system. More precise, we wish to dene a Bayesian model that
follows the physical behaviour of an epidemic while allowing underlying parameters to
track political changes. Such a model would be able to estimate the prevalence whilst
adjusting to changing surroundings and contain uncertainty estimates as a part of its
denition. The COVID-19 epidemic is inherently stochastic and with governmental
regulations being based on, perhaps not worst case, then at least "bad" case, it is
valuable to have uncertainty estimates.
Chapter 2 will introduce the reader to stochastic processes and dene the mathemat-
ical problem of evaluating high-dimensional mixed-eects models. We then present
the necessary tools used to overcome this problem.
In Chapter 3 we start by constructing a mixed-eect model that only consists of posi-
tives and number of test. Then we introduce hospitalizations and then data associated
with B.1.1.7.
In Chapter 4 we expand the stochastic process, that denes the growth rate, to have
non-Gaussian increments.
Chapter 5 presents all the results obtained through our work in this thesis. This chap-
ter also contains a discussion regarding the legitimacy and potential shortcomings in
our model denition.

Chapter 2
Theory - Stochastic Calculus
A stochastic process can be dened as a collection of random variables
{X(t) : t ∈T},
on the probability space (Ω, F, P). t typically represent time and T is the time domain.
This thesis will only consider stochastic processes which take values in Rn. For a xed
time t ∈T we have Xt : Ω→Rn i.e Xt is just a random variable. Alternatively, we
can x the realization ω ∈Ωwhich denes a sample path t 7→Xt(ω). So a stochastic
process can be dened as a probability measure on the space of functions T 7→Rn.
A useful result in stochastic calculus is the denition of the Wiener process.
Denition 2.0.1 (Wiener process) A stochastic process {W(t) : t ≥0} is called a
Wiener process if:
i: W(0) = 0
ii: The increments of {W(t) : t ≥0}
Wt1 −Wt0, Wt2 −Wt−1, · · · , WtN −WtN−1,
are all independent for an arbitrary partition 0 ≤t0 < t1 < · · · ≤tN
iii: W(t + s) −W(s) ∼N(0, t),
∀t, s ≥0
A stochastic dierential equation (SDE) is usually dened on the form
dXt = f(Xt, t)dt
|
{z
}
Drift
+ g(Xt, t)dWt
|
{z
}
Diusion
.
(2.1)
Xt is a continuous stochastic process on some standard probability space (Ω, F, P),
Wt is a Wiener process dened according to denition 2.0.1. In 1-dimension, f, g :
R 7→R are both non-linear functions. f(Xt, t)dt is often referred to as the drift and
g(Xt, t)dWt as the diusion.
2.1
Bayesian inference in SDE's
Let {Xt : t ≥0} be a continuous stochastic process that denes some kind of system.
It could be the price of a stock, the temperature at a certain location or the prevalence
of a virus, which is the case in this thesis. Assume that it follows
dXt = f(Xt, t)dt + g(Xt, t)dWt,
(2.2)
==PAGE:12==

where f and g are usually based on some general knowledge about the dynamics that
govern the system of interest. Let {Yi : i = 0, · · · , N} be a number of observations,
with Yi being measured at time ti
0 ≤t1 < · · · < tN = T.
(2.3)
{Xt : t ≥0} is a continuous process but can be sub-sampled to match the times
of measurements. This denes a Markov process {Xti : i = 0, · · · , N}. State-space
estimation of {Xti} can done with known methods such as the Kalman lter when 2.2
is linear or the unscented Kalman lter, when f(Xt, t) is non-linear.
An alternative way, is to consider the system as a mixed-eects model where {Xti}
are random eects. This allow us to do inference over states and system parameters
θ simultaneously.
Mixed eects model
Let the conditional density of Yi given Xti be denoted as fYi|Xti(y|x). At time ti the
observation Yi is a known quantity thus for every value x the likelihood is known.
This is dened
li(x) = fYi|Xti(y|x)
(2.4)
and is referred to as the state likelihood.
Let p(ti 7→ti+1, xti 7→xti+1) be the transition probabilities of going from xti to xti+1
between time ti and ti+1 in 2.2. In theory, p is found by solving the Fokker-Planck
equation
∂
∂tp(x, t) = −∂
∂x[f(x, t)p(x, t)] + ∂2
∂x2 [D(x, t)p(x, t)],
(2.5)
with diusion coecient D(Xt, t) = g(Xt, t)2/2. p(x, t) denotes the probability den-
sity of x and solving it with initial condition p(x, 0) = fXti(x) denes the evolution
of fXti(x) in respect to time.
Unfortunately, it can only be solved analytically in special cases so it is often neces-
sary to use numerical schemes such as Euler-Maruyama, see section 2.2.1. With this
we dene the joint probability density of all states and observations as
φ(xt0, xt−1, · · · , xtN , y0, y1, · · · , yN; θ) and is calculated as
φ = ψ0(xt0) ·
N

p(ti−1 7→ti, xti−1 7→xti) · li(xi)
(2.6)
where the arguments for φ() are omitted for simplicity. The likelihood of the system
parameters L(θ) is obtained by integrating {Xti} out.
L(θ) =

where X is the state-space of {Xti}. The maximum likelihood estimate of θ is

(2.8)
In general 2.7 does not have a tractable solution due to the integral being high di-
mensional. Luckily L(θ) can be estimated with the Laplace approximation.

==PAGE:14==

The Laplace approximation is widely used on models such as non-linear mixed
eect models or complex state-space models.
The R-package TMB is developed
by Kasper Kristensen and does exactly this. The user passes the joint probability
density φ(xt, θ) through a cpp-le and TMB returns the objective −log L∗(θ) and
its derivates through the use of automatic dierentiation. These can then be used
with a non-linear optimizer to estimate the parameters θ. More details on TMB can
be found through Kristensen et al. 2016.
2.1.3
Multi dimensionality
The previous results were made with {Xt : t ≥0} being a 1-dimensional system,
however, this not a requirement. It is straightforward to show similar results when
{Xt ∈RN : t ≥0}. In addition, it is not necessary for the observations {Yi ∈RM :
t ≥0} to have same dimensions as the system.
2.2
Numerical methods
As mentioned earlier, it is often impossible to solve the Fokker-Planck equation ana-
lytically and it will therefore have to be approximated with numerical methods.
Consider the 1-dimensional stochastic dierential equation
dXt = f(Xt, t)dt

+ g(Xt, t)dWt
|
{z

,
(2.18)
where f, g : R 7→R are both non-linear functions
2.2.1
Euler-Maruyama
The true solution Xt can be approximated with a Markov chain Yt that follows:
 Partition the interval [0, T] into N equal subintervals of width ∆t = T
N where
0 = τ0 < τ1 < · · · < τN = T
 Y0 = x0
 Yn+1 can recursively be updated with: Yn+1 = Yn+f(Yn, τn)∆t+g(Yn, τn)∆Wn
The Euler-Maruyama approximation has strong convergence order p = 1
2 for appropri-
ate SDE's. In stochastic literature an approximation is said to have strong convergence
if e∆t →0, as ∆t →0 where
e∆t :=
sup
0≤tn≤T
E[|Yn −Xtn|].
(2.19)
Having order p = 1
2 means that the error decrease with rate 1
4 when ∆t is halved.
However, when g(Xt, t) is constant then Euler-Maruyama has strong order p = 1.1

Chapter 3
Mixed eects model of Covid-19
epidemic
This chapter is the main part of this thesis and focuses on model denition. First we
dene a simple model that uses tests and positives. Then later we will include hos-
pitalizations and lastly include information regarding the new SARS-CoV-2 variant,
B.1.1.7.
3.1
Data
3.1.1
Positive test and test intensity
Let {Ti ∈Z : i = 0, · · · , N} and {Pi ∈Z : i = 0, · · · , N} dene two time-series with
observations. Ti is the total number of PCR-tests, also called Covid-tests, on a given
day in Denmark. Pi is the number of positive tests so Pi ≤Ti, ∀i. The observations
are taken at
t0 ≤t1 ≤· · · ≤tN = T,
(3.1)
where ti corresponds to day i.
PCR
RT-PCR (Reverse transcription polymerase chain reaction) is a technique that uses
reverse transcription of RNA to DNA and then amplies specic target DNAs using
PCR. This rapidly creates millions of copies which then can be used for analysis.
(Tsang et al. 2021) showed dierent sensitivity depending on the clinical sample tech-
nique. Nasal swab had 82% and Throat swab had 68% sensitivity in detecting SARS-
CoV-2. However, this thesis will not consider the inaccuracies in regard to sample
technique.
Assumption 1 Each person which is tested positive, is in fact infected by SARS-
CoV-2.
The data is provided by SSI (Statens Serum Institut)1 and can be found through
the website. The early measurements of Ti and Pi are considered unreliable due to
non-existing testing capacity and is therefore removed from data. In addition, the
two last days are skipped since all tests have not been analysed yet. The number of
positive tests can be seen with orange dots in Figure 3.1a and the number of tests is
visualized in Figure 3.1b.

3.1.2
Hospitalizations
Apart from the number of positive tests it is also interesting to consider the number
of hospitalizations caused by COVID-19.
Denition 3.1.1 A hospitalization is counted as being caused by COVID-19, if a
subject is admitted to the hospital within 14 days of being tested positive for SARS-
CoV-2.
The total number of newly admitted per day is dened as a discrete time-series {Hi ∈
Z, i = 0, 1, · · · , M}. The earliest observation t0 is d. 01 Marts 2020. Hi is illustrated
by the blue dots in Figure 3.1a.
3.1.3
Remarks
It is interesting how Pi and Hi are highly correlated after July 2020 but not as corre-
lated in the beginning of the epidemic. It is clearly due to Pi being closely tied to the
test intensity Ti where as Hi is not tied to the test intensity at all. More tests lead
to more people testing positive. This means that when Ti is low we will see Hi being
relatively higher than Pi which equalizes as Ti increases. This raises an interesting
and important question, namely how to take the changing test intensity into account
when trying to model the spread of the epidemic through Pi?
==PAGE:17==
3.2. Modelling of test and positives
9
3.2
Modelling of test and positives
This section focuses on constructing an appropriate model that captures the underly-
ing dynamics in the current epidemic. Initially we will consider a deterministic model
that is often used when trying to model epidemic behaviour. Then we will make the
necessary adjustments and introduce stochastic eects.
SIR - Compartmental model
A compartmental model works by dividing the population into dierent compartments
with certain labels and then dening the transmission rates between each compart-
ment.
The simplest version is the SIR model which has 3 groups (Susceptible, Infectious,
Recovered) that follows the set of ordinary dierential equations
dS
dt = −βIS
N ,
dI
dt = βIS
N
−γI,
(3.2)
dR
dt = γI.
S is the number of people that are susceptible to receiving the virus, I is the number
of infected individuals that are capable of transmitting the virus and R is the number
of removed individuals. R is based on the assumption that after an individual has
recovered from an infection, it has gained immunity which makes it impossible to
be infected again. N is a constant and is dened as the sum of S, I and R thus
corresponding to the total population.
β can be interpreted as how infectious a certain virus is. When β is high then the
virus is easily transmitted between individuals whereas a low value would mean that
it is dicult for the virus to infect new individuals. γ is the rate by which people
that has been infected with a virus recover. Alternatively can it be dened as γ = 1
D
where D is the average time it takes someone to recover. If γ is low then the recovery
rate is slow and the opposite if γ is high.
βIS
N
is the rate at which new people get
infected and γI is the rate of people that recover from being infected.
Specications
The standard denition of 3.2 does not suit our problem, which is to estimate the
infection rate of SARS-CoV-2 among Danish citizens. It is adjusted with the set of
assumptions
Assumption 2 (Model assumptions) :
a: S is assumed to be constant on the given period
b: β(t) is time dependent.
Assumption
2.a): The total number of infected is relatively low compared to the
total population size. As of d. 15 April 2021 the total number of people in Den-
mark that has been documented as being infected by SARS-CoV-2 is 240.330 out of
4.674.602. This is equivalent to S
N = 0.948 whereas it was 1 in the beginning of the
epidemic. Consequently, this also means that individuals are not considered immune
after being infected.
==PAGE:18==
10
Chapter 3. Mixed eects model of Covid-19 epidemic
Assumption 2.b): The infection-rate β can be considered as consisting of two ele-
ments: The genetic composition that makes the virus transmissible and the behaviour
of its hosts. By disregarding mutations, the genetic composition can be considered
constant however, peoples behaviour cannot. By limiting social interaction, wearing
masks etc. the infection-rate β can be lowered. It is therefore a necessary assumption,
that β depend on time.
The two assumptions simplify 3.2 to one ODE
dI
dt = β(t)IS −γI,
(3.3)
where N is omitted for simplicity.
3.2.1
Introducing stochastic eects
It is the number of infected.
Each one of the infected has a random number of
encounters with susceptible people on a daily basis and each of those encounters has
a certain probability of a successful infection.
This makes It an inherently random process thus should also be modelled as one to
gain the most precise results.
3.3 can be expressed as a dierence equation
dIt = β(t)ItStdt −γItdt,
(3.4)
with dIt being the dierence It+dt −It over a short time period [t, t + dt). Up to this
point It ∈Z is the number of infected but will henceforth be dened as the prevalence
It
N . It is assumed that N is large enough so It ∈R. 3.4 is turned into a stochastic
dierential equation by adding multiplicative noise
dIt = rtItdt + σIItdWt,
(3.5)
where Wt is a standard 1-dimensional Wiener process. rt = βtS −γ has been used to
simplify the expression.
When dealing with stochastic dierential equations it is typically preferable to have
state independent noise since it makes numerical approximations more reliable. We
introduce the following theorem Øksendal 2003
Theorem 1 (Itô's lemma) : Let Xt be a stochastic process
dXt = f(Xt, t)dt + g(Xt, t)dWt.
(3.6)
Let ψ(Xt, t) ∈C2[0,∞) × R. Then
Zt = ψ(Xt, t)
(3.7)
is also a stochastic process

3.2.2
Growth rate
From now on r(t) = β(t)S −γ will be referred to as the growth rate. The growth rate
is the main factor that controls It, so when rt is positive It will tend to increase and
decrease when rt is negative. rt is assumed to follow a Stochastic dierential equation
which will be govern by a scaled Wiener process, also called a random walk
drt = σrdWt.
(3.10)
It should be noted that γ has to be estimated from dierent sources to insure that
rt has a unique solution. From (Ue Høgsbro Thygesen, personal communication),
γ =
1
4.7 which will be used in the remaining thesis. Arguments can be made, whether
a random walk with normal distributed increments is a good approximation to the
real process and this will be discussed in further details later.
3.2.3
Observation of positives
It is obvious that Pi is highly dependent on Ti but the exact relationship between
the two is not obvious. Intuition says, that when testing few people then the relative
probability of being positive should be higher, because when only few are tested, it is
expected to mainly be those with symptoms. The following model is proposed
Pi ∼Poisson(ItiT β
i ),
(3.11)
where β ∈[0, 1]. To provide context: β = 1 when test-samples are selected randomly.
So double the tests lead to twice the number of positives, i.e a linear relation. β = 0
would mean that everyone that is infected will be found and tested thus a change in
Ti would have no eect on the expected number of positives.
3.2.4
Model with discrete steps
All the essential elements that are needed to construct the initial model have now
been introduced.
The overall model structure is illustrated in Figure 3.2.
rt, It
are continuous processes but will only be evaluated at discrete time points ti which
is portrayed with the squares.
The circles indicate the observation process.
The
vertical arrows specify the information that is transferred between each process and
the horizontal arrows dene one discrete time-step update. In theory, each of these
updates is found by solving the Fokker-Planck equation. Unfortunately, it can only be
solved analytically in special cases so it is often necessary to use numerical schemes.

Remarks
Rather than letting σI and σr be estimated independently we introduce a new param-
eter
τ = σI
σr
.
(3.15)
τ can be understood as a smoothing parameter that dene to what extent uctuations
should be considered changes in the growth rate. If τ is low, then rt will have a lot
of noise and therefore consider daily uctuations as changes in growth rate. On the
other hand, if τ is high, then rt will have low variance and will only consider larger
uctuations, over longer time periods, as changes in growth rate.

Results

displays the growth rate rt, when τ is free and xed respectively. Despite signicant
dierence in likelihood, it is deemed unrealistic that τ should be free, since rt reacts
too slow. We contribute this to model:3.14 being a lacking representation of the true
system and it is therefore necessary to x τ, so that the remaining results have an
understandable interpretation.

Reproduction number
From epidemiology the reproduction number R is often used as it provides an easy
way of rating a virus' ability to spread. In more detail, R is the expected number
of secondary infections from one infected host. So if R = 2 then every person that
is infected will on average infect two new people. That means when R > 1 then the
epidemic increases while it decreases for R < 1.
This reproduction number can be calculated as
Rt = γ−1 · rt + 1.
(3.16)
This gives a estimate of Rt at every time step and is illustrated in Figure 3.4
Noticeable, Rt is low in the periods April-June 2020 and Januar 2021 - middle of
February 2021, possibly in response to the prevalence being high in the months prior
3.3a. We also see Rt is at its highest between July and September which is perhaps not
too surprising, considering vacations and increased tendency to meet up with friends
and family.

3.3. Hospitalizations

3.3
Hospitalizations
In the previous section we considered Pi, the number of positive tests, to track the
development of SARS-CoV-2. Another important factor to consider is how severe
the virus is. A virus with low severity can basically be ignored regardless of how
transmissible it is. To understand the severity we will consider the number of people
that are admitted to the hospital due to Covid 19. Those that need medical care
to treat the virus is also the group with highest risk of suering long-term eects or
in worst case dying (Romero Starke et al. 2020). It is important to insure that the
necessary resources are available and that the healthcare capacity is not exceeded. A
loose lockdown-policy might result in an uncontrollable growth in Pi and consequently
Hi. On the other hand, a strict policy might hamper society and lead to unutilized
resources in the healthcare section. Hence it is crucial to understand the elements
that control Hi and how it is connected to the prevalence It.
In addition to the society benets it is also useful to consider Hi from a modelling
perspective. The number of people that are admitted to the hospital are not dependent
on number of tests so there is no need for test-intensity-correction thus a possible more
precise portray in the development of SARS-CoV-2.
3.3.1
Observation of Hospitalizations
Let {ξt : t ≥0} be a stochastic process that is dened as a scaled Wiener process
dξt = σξdWt,
(3.17)
and let f(Iti, ξti, η), η ∈R be a non-linear function that dene the expected number
of hospitalizations E[Hi]. The likelihood contribution of each element in {Hi : i =
0, 1, · · · , N} is conditioned on f(Iti, ξti, η)
P(Hi|f(Iti, ξti, η)).
(3.18)
Each observation in Hi is the number of newly admitted, which is a count of instances
over a xed period ∆t, thus it is a reasonable assumption that it will follow a Poisson
distribution
Hi|Iti, ξti, τ ∼Pois (f(Iti, ξti, η)) .
(3.19)
It is important to understand the time dynamic and possible delay between getting
tested positive tI and being admitted to the hospital tH. This dierence ∆t = tI −
tH is likely not constant and might vary for each individual. Let h(It, ξt) and g(η)
denote two non-linear functions. It is assumed that f(It, ξt, η) can be expressed as
the convolution
f(It, ξt, η) = (h ∗g)(t) :=
Z ∞
−∞
h(It−τ, ξt−τ)g(η, τ)dτ.
(3.20)
This denition allows for the time and rate aspect to be considered independently of
each other.
Time function
g(η, t) can be understood as a time function that models the time relationship between
the prevalence It and hospitalizations Hi. An example of a possible function choice
for g(η, t) is
g(η, t) = δ(t −η)
(3.21)

Hi ∼Pois(h(Iti−η, ξti−η)).

Another example is to let g(η, t) follow a probability density function. It could dened
as a theoretical distribution or it could be a density that is based on measured data.
An example of this is the density in Figure 3.5. It is based on data from SSI (Statens
Serum Insitut) and describe the dierence in days between being conrmed positive
for SARS-CoV-2 and being admitted to the hospital. x = 0 means that an individual
tested positive for SARS-CoV-2 the same day that they were admitted to the hospital.
x = −3 means that the person tested positive 3 days prior to being admitted and
similarly x = 3 means that the subject tested positive 3 days post being admitted.

h(It, ξt) = Iteξt
(3.23)
The motivation behind 3.23 is that the expected number of hospitalizations is closely
related to the prevalence It. Though it is also recognized that the relation is not
constant and might vary over time.
This variation, that is dened as eξt can be
understood as a relative ratio between prevalence and hospitalizations. If It is constant

a constant number of days compared to It and ξt. Also η = 2 makes intuitive sense
since one would expect a person to test positive initially and then seek medical care

3.4
New SARS-CoV-2 variant, B.1.1.7
VOC 202012/01 (Variant of Concern year 2020, month 12, variant 01), also referred to
as B.1.1.7, is a variant of SARS-CoV-2 that originates from The United Kingdom. It
initially appeared in the South East England and quickly replaced other virus lineages
in the same area. As of the 08. Marts 2021 a study showed that 113 countries or
territories had reported cases of B.1.1.7. Among those, there are 19 countries that
show evidence for community transmission which refer to the situation where positive
cases can no longer be traced back to imported cases.
A preliminary analysis from UK suggests that B.1.1.7 has increased transmissibility.
The analysis also shows no change, in disease severity (by length of hospitalization
and 28-day case fatality) or occurrence of reinfections compared to other SARS-CoV-2
viruses.2 Another report from UK published d.11 Februar 2021 present evidence that
B.1.1.7 has increased disease severity compared to other virus variants.3
A study from SSI (Statens Serum Institut) (Bager et al. 2021) published d. 02 Marts
2021, concluded that lineage B.1.1.7 was associated with an increased risk of hospi-
talization compared to other lineages. The study modelled the odds ratio (OR) of
admission for infection B.1.1.7 compared the other lineages using a logistic regression
model. Data consisted of 35.887 individuals that tested postive for SARS-CoV-2 and
23,057 had WGS (Whole genome sequencing) performed. 18,499 resulted in a viral
genome and 2.155 were B.1.1.7. Results showed an adjusted OR of 1.64 (95%CI 1.32-
2.04) for admission among those with genome B.1.1.7..
This raises two important question: How to model the total prevalence It with mul-
tiple variants that has potentially dierent transmission rates? And does the model
result show evidence that support the claim that B.1.1.7 is associated with increased
risk of hospitalization?
3.4.1
Ratio of B.1.1.7 instances
Data is published on a weekly basis where a portion of the total number of positive
tests Pi are selected for WGS. Here they are tested to see if they belong to the B.1.1.7
variant. Figure 3.9 shows the number of positive tests for B.1.1.7 on a weekly basis
compared to the wild types. The gure heavily support the claim that B.1.1.7 has
increased transmissibility. The latest data suggest that B.1.1.7 currently makes up
93.2% of total number of cases.
Incorporate B.1.1.7
Let I2(t) be a stochastic process that dene the prevalence of B.1.1.7 and I1(t) be the
prevalence of the remaining wild types such that I(ti) = I1(ti) + I2(ti). It is assumed
that both processes follow a stochastic dierential equation that is equal to the one
presented in 3.5
dI1(t) = r1(t)I1(t)dt + σII(t)dW1,
(3.26)
dI2(t) = r2(t)I2(t)dt + σII(t)dW2,
(3.27)
2https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/
attachment_data/file/959361/Technical_Briefing_VOC202012-2_Briefing_2.pdf
3https://www.gov.uk/government/publications/nervtag-update-note-on-b117-severity-11-february-2021

Figure 3.9: Number of positives from those with complete genome
sequence
where W1(t) and W2(t) are both Wiener processes and they are assumed to be inde-
pendent. It is also assumed that both processes share the same noise parameter σI.
The growth rate for each process:
r1(t) = β1(t)S −γ
(3.28)
r2(t) = β2(t)S −γ.
(3.29)
It is assumed that β2(t) = ωβ1(t) with ω ∈R+. This means that r2(t) is not its own
random process but rather a scaled version of r1(t)
r2(t) = ω(r1(t) + γ) −γ.
(3.30)
Recall that the growth rate r(t) is aected by multiple external factors, such as govern-
mental intervention, peoples perception and seasonal patterns. All dierent variants
should be subjected to these eects and that is why r2(t) is a scaled version of r1(t).
In addition we avoid estimating another random process which helps reduce the model
complexity and thus increase statistical power.
It is not clear whether a linear relation β2(t) = ωβ1(t) is sucient. Consider a sit-
uation where everyone is acting so recklessly that every contact with an individual
that is infected will lead to an infection. In such a situation it does not matter if
one mutation is more infectious than another thus β1(t) = β2(t). However it is very
unlikely that such a situation would occur hence β2(t) = ωβ1(t) is likely sucient.
3.4.2
Observation process with B.1.1.7 ratio
Let SB117(ti) be the total number of positive tests, where WGS was performed and
it resulted in a viral genome, on a weekly basis. Then let PB117(ti) be the number of
samples in SB117(ti) that tested positive for B.1.1.7. Each sample in SB117(ti) can be
understood as an independent trail with a certain probability ρ of testing positive for
B.1.1.7 and 1 −ρ of being negative.

PB117(ti) ∼B(SB117(ti), ρti).
(3.31)
The random process I2(t) is the prevalence of B.1.1.7 thus it is straightforward to
dene
ρti =
I2(ti)
I1(ti) + I2(ti).
(3.32)
3.4.3
Model with B.1.1.7 variant
The stochastic dierential equation that governs I2(t) is iteratively updated using the
Euler-Maruyama. Each increment follows a normal distribution
log I2(ti+1)|I2(ti), r1(ti) ∼N(log I2(ti) + r2(ti), σ2
I).
(3.33)
The number of samples SB117(ti) and positive tests PB117(ti), are including through
the observation process 3.31. This is added to the previous model denition:

3.4.5
Multiple variants
The model is only expanded to incorporate B.1.1.7 but it is easy to extend this ap-
proach and incorporate numerous variants. Consider an example where we would like
to model n-variants.
Let It = [I1(t), I2(t), · · · , In(t)] and rt = [r1(t), r2(t), · · · , rn(t)] be row vectors. Then
dIt = rtItdt + ΣIItdWt
(3.36)
with ΣI ∈R(n,n) being the covariance matrix and Wt is an n-dimensional Wiener
process. The growth rate is dened as
rt = ω(rt + γ) −γ
(3.37)
where ω = [1, ω2, · · · , ωn] ∈Rn−1
+
. Let ρti ∈Rn
[0,1] where R[0,1] = {x ∈R|0 ≤x ≤1},
be the ratio of each estimate
ρti =
Iti
Pn
i=1 Ii(ti),
(3.38)
where the likelihood contribution for each variant is dened with a binomial distribu-
tion as in 3.31.
This is a strong extension as it is easy to modify the model to include/discard variants
as they become evident or die out.
==PAGE:33==
25
Chapter 4
Hyperbolic distributed growth rate
As it has being discussed earlier is the growth rate rt mainly inuenced by two external
factors: Government intervention and personal perception. Personal perception can
gradually change over time i.e people become more or less afraid of the virus which
result in them changing their behaviour. Governmental intervention can have enor-
mous and instant eect on the transmission rate as it has been seen with nationwide
lockdowns. It might be hard for the model to estimate these instantaneous changes
with a normal distribution and perhaps another distribution is more appropriate.
4.1
Hyperbolic distribution
The hyperbolic distribution H(ζ, β, δ, µ) is continuous with PDF

the center-location and β is the skewness parameter. Our distribution must lie around
zero and for simplicity we will not consider a skrewed distribution so µ = 0, β = 0.
This simplies 4.1

4.3
Bias in Laplace Approximation
In section: 2.1 the goal was to evaluate the likelihood L(θ) for a set of model param-
eters θ. The Laplace approximation was used, to avoid integrating all random states
xt out of the joint probability density function φ(xt, θ). The approximation assume
that φ(xt, θ), is reasonable normal distributed around its mode, where the mode refer
to the optimal random states for a given set of parameters θ.
In model: 3.34, the random states are xt = [I1(t), I2(t), ξt, r1(t)] and the model param-
eters are θ = [β, σI1, τ, σξ, ω, ζ]. As ζ →0 the increments ∆r1(ti) = r1(ti+1) −r1(ti)
starts to follow a double-sided exponential distribution. As a result the joint density
φ(xt, θ) os less Gaussian around its mode ˆxt, so the assumption is less appropriate.
This leads to the following conjecture.
Conjecture 2 The Laplace approximation becomes less accurate when ζ →0 and as
a result, TMB is biased towards higher values of ζ
4.3.1
Stan - NUTS sampler
Stan is a C++ library that is used in Bayesian modelling and inference. NUTS (No-
U-turn sampler) is an adaptive variant of the well known Hamiltonian Monte Carlo
algorithm. NUTS focuses on automatically optimizing the number of leapfrog steps
in each iteration to insure high eciency. A precise denition of the NUTS algorithm
can be found in (Homan and Gelman 2014) and (Homan and Gelman 2011).
NUTS does Bayesian inference on the full posterior p(θ, xt) i.e in addition to θ, it
treats every random state xt as a parameter that needs to be estimated. It does also
have the option to use the Laplace approximation to remove xt from the posterior p(θ).
==PAGE:37==
4.3. Bias in Laplace Approximation
29
4.3.2
Posterior samples
To illustrate a potential bias with the Laplace approximation we will consider two
situations, one where ζ = e5 and one where ζ = e−3. For each ζ, we run NUTS
algorithm twice, one with the Laplace approximation and one without. Each run is
compared by looking at the posterior-samples. If the approximation is accurate then
the samples will be similar and they will dierent if the approximation is bad. In
addition, τ = 7 is xed in both situations.
Figure 4.3 shows the sample density for each parameter in θ when ζ = e−3. W notice
a clear dierence in mean value for β and log σI dependent on whether the Laplace
approximation is used or not.
The same goes for ω where the dierence is large
between each density. There is only a minor dierence in log σξ.

Chapter 5
Discussion and Results
This chapter presents the results generated by the nal model 4.11. This section will
also include a discussion of the results. From Figure 4.6, it is obvious that log ζ has
no optimal value and it will therefore have to xed for us to be able analyse the un-
derlying random processes: I1(t), I2(t), r1(t) and ξt. It is xed at log ζ = −2. The
remaining system parameters θ = [β, σI, σξ, ω] are estimated with NUTS where we do
not use the Laplace approximation due to the bias that was proved in section 4.3. The
maximum likelihood estimates of the system parameters can be found in Table 5.1.
Figure 5.1 shows the total prevalence It = I1(t) + I2(t) and the positive percentage

of tests Pi
Ti . A naive approach to estimating the prevalence would be with the posi-
tive percentage. However, it might result in wrong conclusions which is apparent in
Figure 5.1. Based on the positive percentage, the rst outbreak seems more extreme
than the second (The one around December). Though looking at It the opposite is
concluded, namely that the second outbreak was more serious. We notice how the
prevalence gradually increases in Autumn 2020. Then we see an explosion in the end
of November 2020 and in 3 weeks the prevalence more than doubles. It is likely con-
nected to Christmas shopping and increased social interaction.
Figure 5.2 shows the estimated reproduction number Rt. We notice a slow increase
around May and June 2020 which corresponds nicely to phase 2 in the reopening of
Denmark 1.
Then we see a large shift in Rt around the start of July 2020. It could be explained
with the summer holidays which typically lies around July. However, there are no
data to support this claim and it is purely speculation.
Around December 2020, the reproduction number decreases and can be explained
with the numerous restrictions that was implemented throughout December 2020 2

In the middle of January 2021, Rt starts to increase until it fades out in the middle of
March and slightly decreases. This movement is most likely due to the introduction
of B.1.1.7 and how it has progressively taken over as the most dominant type. This
reproduction number is also compared to the one estimated by SSI (Statens Serum
Institute) Figure 5.3 5. One clear dierence is around August where it quickly dips
below 1. Another dierence is in the recent estimates. Our model estimates the cur-
rent reproduction number to 1.017 where SSI's estimate is slightly below 1. So we
predict the epidemic to be growing where SSI suggests that it is decreasing.

34
Chapter 5. Discussion and Results
Figure 5.3: Reproduction taken from SSI's website. The quality is
low since I had to screenshot it
In Figure 5.4 we see eξti and the ratio of positive tests that caused by the B.1.1.7
variant. Recall that eξti denes a relative ratio of hospitalizations Hi compared to
prevalence It. This means that if eξti is doubled then the probability of being admitted
to the hospital after being infected is twice as high. We notice how it is low during the
months July - October 2020. This supports the idea that the virus was primary car-
ried by young people6 which has a lower probability of developing severe symptoms.
We notice a large increase around December and January. A possible explanation is
that elders are more likely to participate in social gatherings such as Christmas Eve.
And with elders being more susceptible to be hospitalized if they get infected. eξti
spikes around 85 in the middle of January 2021 which is almost 4.5 times the value
that it was in the start of December. This means that people that were infected in
January 2021 were 4.5 times as likely to be hospitalized. In the last months, middle
of February - middle of April 2021, it has stabilized around 40-50 which is still 2.5
times the value it was before December. It is presumably also tied to B.1.1.7. As it
was mentioned in section:3.3, studies suggest that B.1.1.7 has increased severity which
would explain why eξti have not returned to the level it was before December.
Denmark has begun distributing vaccinations and as of April 15. 977.000 have started
the vaccine program and of those 461.000 have nished. It can also be seen in Fig-
ure 5.5 illustrating the development. Vaccines are provided to the vulnerable part
and those that work with this group rst, so expectations are that eξti will decrease
when more vaccines are distributed. So far there are no indication of this happening,
though it is still very early on in the process with only 7.69% having nished the
vaccine program.
From Table 5.1 we see ω = 1.34 (1.18 −1.5) which means that SARS-CoV-2 variant
B.1.1.7 has 34% increased transmission rate compared to the local wild types.
Consider the observation process for Pi that was dened in Section 3.2.3
Pi ∼Pois(ItiT β

(5.1)
The initial motivation with including β < 1 was that as the test capacity increases the
individual probability of being positive decreases. In the beginning, it was only people

with strong symptoms that were tested, then it was people with less symptoms, then
people that had been in contact with a positive etc. Put in another way, the prior
contribution for each test is getting weaker, as the test capacity increases. So T β
i is
a way of compensating for this behaviour. However, the test capacity is no longer a
limiting factor so the prior does not become weaker as Ti increase. T β
i is therefore not
viable for large enough test capacity and will only be more inaccurate as Ti increase.
==PAGE:44==
36
Chapter 6
Conclusion
We managed to develop a model of the COVID-19 epidemic.
It was not possible
to estimate the true prevalence of SARS-CoV-2 but it was possible to construct a
prevalence-index. This makes it possible to do quantitative comparisons of the current
epidemic state with earlier times. The model can estimate the reproduction number
Rt and can be used to measure the eect of regulations. The reproduction number Rt
roughly follows the changes in regulations, but in the last 4 months despite no change
in regulations, it has increased which is potentially explained by B.1.1.7 that was
found to have 34% higher transmission rate. The current estimate d. 16 April 2021 is
Rt = 1.017, meaning the epidemic is predicted to grow slowly. The model estimates
the rate of being admitted after infection and it has increased 2.5 times since December
2020. We have not been able to detect a change in the rate of hospitalization after
the vaccines have been distributed.
During model development and analysis it was necessary to x certain parameters in
the model. This suggests that certain system dynamics is not explained completely
by the current model.
We also discovered that the Laplace approximation is inaccurate when model consist
of non-Gaussian or equivalent non-linear random processes. It is therefore necessary
to use alternative algorithms that do not rely on the Laplace approximation, in these
cases.
==PAGE:45==
37
Chapter 7
Future Work
The assumption that increments in the growth rate follow a normal distribution is
not sucient. We need a non-Gaussian distribution to capture the non-continuous
eects in the growth rate. However, we found that the Laplace approximation is in-
accurate when a non-Gaussian distribution is used, so it is necessary to use dierent
algorithms. In the thesis, we use NUTS to estimate the shape parameter ζ of a hy-
perbolic distribution but it concludes that ζ has zero eect. Further investigation is
needed to explain this result. Is the hyperbolic distribution inadequate at capturing
the changes in rt or perhaps the model to complex for NUTS?
With an increasing test intensity, we expect the number of "random samples" to in-
crease, so β should be closer to 1. A possible solution is to make T β
i
a piece-wise
function where it is linear for large enough Ti. Though it is dicult task to decide
when such a transition is appropriate and even if it is appropriate at all. More re-
search needs to be put into the eects of an increasing test capacity.
This model does not include deaths but it is an obvious extension. It contains addi-
tional information which is always benecial.
